<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_history">Family history</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tagore_family" title="Tagore family">Tagore family</a></div>
<p>The name Tagore is the anglicised transliteration of <a href="/wiki/Thakur_(title)" title="Thakur (title)">Thakur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasrin_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nasrin-17">[16]</a></sup> The original surname of the <a href="/wiki/Tagore_family" title="Tagore family">Tagores</a> was Kushari. They were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pirali_Brahmin" title="Pirali Brahmin">Pirali Brahmin</a> ('Piraliâ historically carried a stigmatized and pejorative connotation)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> originally belonged to a village named <i>Kush</i> in the district named <a href="/wiki/Bardhaman_district" title="Bardhaman district">Burdwan</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>. The biographer of Rabindranath Tagore, <a href="/wiki/Prabhat_Kumar_Mukhopadhyaya" title="Prabhat Kumar Mukhopadhyaya">Prabhat Kumar Mukhopadhyaya</a> wrote in the first volume of his book <i>Rabindrajibani O Rabindra Sahitya Prabeshak</i> that
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Kusharis were the descendants of Deen Kushari, the son of <a href="/wiki/Bhatta_Narayana" title="Bhatta Narayana">Bhatta Narayana</a>; Deen was granted a village named Kush (in <a href="/wiki/Bardhaman_district" title="Bardhaman district">Burdwan</a> zilla) by <a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">Maharaja</a> Kshitisura, he became its chief and came to be known as Kushari.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tagore1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-tagore1-10">[9]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_events">Life and events</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Early_life:_1861.E2.80.931878"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life:_1861â1878">Early life: 1861â1878</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Early life of Rabindranath Tagore">Early life of Rabindranath Tagore</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1833" data-file-width="1202" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg/220px-Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg/330px-Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg/440px-Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_in_London_1879.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young Tagore in London, 1879</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>The last two days a storm has been raging, similar to the description in my songâ<i>Jhauro jhauro borishe baridhara</i> Â [... amidst it] a hapless, homeless man drenched from top to toe standing on the roof of his steamer [...] the last two days I have been singing this song over and overÂ [...] as a result the pelting sound of the intense rain, the wail of the wind, the sound of the heaving Gorai River, [...] have assumed a fresh life and found a new language and I have felt like a major actor in this new musical drama unfolding before me.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">â Letter to <a href="/wiki/Indira_Devi_Chaudhurani" title="Indira Devi Chaudhurani">Indira Devi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011-20">[19]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The youngest of 13 surviving children, Tagore (nicknamed "Rabi") was born on 7 May 1861 in the <a href="/wiki/Jorasanko_Thakur_Bari" title="Jorasanko Thakur Bari">Jorasanko mansion</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcutta" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobelfacts_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-nobelfacts-21">[20]</a></sup> the son of <a href="/wiki/Debendranath_Tagore" title="Debendranath Tagore">Debendranath Tagore</a> (1817â1905) and Sarada Devi (1830â1875).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[b]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath-Tagore-Mrinalini-Devi-1883.jpg"><img alt="Black-and-white photograph of a finely dressed man and woman: the man, smiling, stands with the hand on the hip and elbow turned outward with a shawl draped over his shoulders and in Bengali formal wear. In front of him, the woman, seated, is in elaborate dress and shawl; she leans against a carved table supporting a vase and flowing leaves." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="352" data-file-width="234" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Rabindranath-Tagore-Mrinalini-Devi-1883.jpg/170px-Rabindranath-Tagore-Mrinalini-Devi-1883.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Rabindranath-Tagore-Mrinalini-Devi-1883.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath-Tagore-Mrinalini-Devi-1883.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tagore and his wife Mrinalini Devi, 1883</div></div></div>
<p>Tagore was raised mostly by servants; his mother had died in his early childhood and his father travelled widely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson192620_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson192620-28">[26]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tagore_family" title="Tagore family">Tagore family</a> was at the forefront of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bengal_renaissance" title="Bengal renaissance">Bengal renaissance</a>. They hosted the publication of literary magazines; theatre and recitals of Bengali and Western classical music featured there regularly. Tagore's father invited several professional <a href="/wiki/Dhrupad" title="Dhrupad">Dhrupad</a> musicians to stay in the house and teach <a href="/wiki/Indian_classical_music" title="Indian classical music">Indian classical music</a> to the children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESom201016_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESom201016-29">[27]</a></sup> Tagore's oldest brother <a href="/wiki/Dwijendranath_Tagore" title="Dwijendranath Tagore">Dwijendranath</a> was a philosopher and poet. Another brother, <a href="/wiki/Satyendranath_Tagore" title="Satyendranath Tagore">Satyendranath</a>, was the first Indian appointed to the elite and formerly all-European <a href="/wiki/Indian_Civil_Service" title="Indian Civil Service">Indian Civil Service</a>. Yet another brother, <a href="/wiki/Jyotirindranath_Tagore" title="Jyotirindranath Tagore">Jyotirindranath</a>, was a musician, composer, and playwright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b10_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b10-30">[28]</a></sup> His sister Swarnakumari became a novelist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> Jyotirindranath's wife <a href="/wiki/Kadambari_Devi" title="Kadambari Devi">Kadambari Devi</a>, slightly older than Tagore, was a dear friend and powerful influence. Her abrupt suicide in 1884, soon after he married, left him profoundly distraught for years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tagore largely avoided classroom schooling and preferred to roam the manor or nearby <a href="/wiki/Bolpur" title="Bolpur">Bolpur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panihati" title="Panihati">Panihati</a>, which the family visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson192621â24_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson192621â24-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDas2009_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDas2009-34">[32]</a></sup> His brother <a href="/wiki/Hemendranath_Tagore" title="Hemendranath Tagore">Hemendranath</a> tutored and physically conditioned himâby having him swim the Ganges or trek through hills, by gymnastics, and by practising judo and wrestling. He learned drawing, anatomy, geography and history, literature, mathematics, Sanskrit, and Englishâhis least favourite subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199548â49_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199548â49-35">[33]</a></sup> Tagore loathed formal educationâhis scholarly travails at the local <a href="/wiki/Presidency_University,_Kolkata" title="Presidency University, Kolkata">Presidency College</a> spanned a single day. Years later he held that proper teaching does not explain things; proper teaching stokes curiosity:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199550_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199550-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>
After his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upanayan" title="Upanayan">upanayan</a></i> (coming-of-age rite) at age eleven, Tagore and his father left Calcutta in February 1873 to tour India for several months, visiting his father's <a href="/wiki/Santiniketan" title="Santiniketan">Santiniketan</a> estate and <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a> before reaching the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayan</a> <a href="/wiki/Hill_station" title="Hill station">hill station</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dalhousie,_India" title="Dalhousie, India">Dalhousie</a>. There Tagore read biographies, studied history, astronomy, modern science, and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, and examined the classical poetry of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%81lid%C4%81sa" title="KÄlidÄsa">KÄlidÄsa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199555â56_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199555â56-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart_2003_91_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart_2003_91-38">[36]</a></sup> During his 1-month stay at Amritsar in 1873 he was greatly influenced by melodious <a href="/wiki/Gurbani" title="Gurbani">gurbani</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nanak_bani&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nanak bani (page does not exist)">nanak bani</a> being sung at Golden Temple for which both father and son were regular visitors. He mentions about this in his <i>My Reminiscences</i> (1912)<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The golden temple of Amritsar comes back to me like a dream. Many a morning have I accompanied my father to this Gurudarbar of the Sikhs in the middle of the lake. There the sacred chanting resounds continually. My father, seated amidst the throng of worshippers, would sometimes add his voice to the hymn of praise, and finding a stranger joining in their devotions they would wax enthusiastically cordial, and we would return loaded with the sanctified offerings of sugar crystals and other sweets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> He wrote 6 poems relating to Sikhism and a number of articles in Bengali children's magazine about Sikhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Tagore returned to Jorosanko and completed a set of major works by 1877, one of them a long poem in the <a href="/wiki/Maithili_language" title="Maithili language">Maithili</a> style of <a href="/wiki/Vidyapati" title="Vidyapati">Vidyapati</a>. As a joke, he claimed that these were the lost works of newly discovered 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaiá¹£á¹ava</a> poet BhÄnusiá¹ha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart_2003_3_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart_2003_3-41">[39]</a></sup> Regional experts accepted them as the lost works of the fictitious poet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell20033_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell20033-42">[40]</a></sup> He debuted in the short-story genre in Bengali with "<a href="/wiki/Bhikharini" title="Bhikharini">Bhikharini</a>" ("The Beggar Woman").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196145_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196145-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b265_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b265-44">[42]</a></sup> Published in the same year, <i>Sandhya Sangit</i> (1882) includes the poem "Nirjharer Swapnabhanga" ("The Rousing of the Waterfall").
</p><h3><span id="Shelaidaha:_1878.E2.80.931901"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Shelaidaha:_1878â1901">Shelaidaha: 1878â1901</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2403" data-file-width="3942" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg/220px-%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg/330px-%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg/440px-%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E0%A6%B0%E0%A6%AC%E0%A7%80%E0%A6%A8%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%A6%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%B0_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%A0%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AC%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%A1%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BF_%E0%A6%B6%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%B2%E0%A6%BE%E0%A6%87%E0%A6%A6%E0%A6%B9_%E0%A6%95%E0%A7%81%E0%A6%B7%E0%A7%8D%E0%A6%9F%E0%A6%BF%E0%A6%AF%E0%A6%BC%E0%A6%BE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tagore's house in <a href="/wiki/Shilaidaha" title="Shilaidaha">Shilaidaha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a></div></div></div>
<p>Because Debendranath wanted his son to become a barrister, Tagore enrolled at a public school in Brighton, East Sussex, England in 1878.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011-20">[19]</a></sup> He stayed for several months at a house that the Tagore family owned near <a href="/wiki/Brighton" title="Brighton">Brighton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hove" title="Hove">Hove</a>, in Medina Villas; in 1877 his nephew and nieceâSuren and <a href="/wiki/Indira_Devi_Chaudhurani" title="Indira Devi Chaudhurani">Indira Devi</a>, the children of Tagore's brother <a href="/wiki/Satyendranath_Tagore" title="Satyendranath Tagore">Satyendranath</a>âwere sent together with their mother, Tagore's sister-in-law, to live with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199568_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199568-45">[43]</a></sup> He briefly read law at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a>, but again left school, opting instead for independent study of <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_plays" title="Shakespeare's plays">Shakespeare's plays</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Coriolanus" title="Coriolanus">Coriolanus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Antony_and_Cleopatra" title="Antony and Cleopatra">Antony and Cleopatra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Religio_Medici" title="Religio Medici">Religio Medici</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">Thomas Browne</a>.</i> Lively English, Irish, and Scottish folk tunes impressed Tagore, whose own tradition of <a href="/wiki/Nidhu_Babu" title="Nidhu Babu">Nidhubabu</a>-authored <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kirtans" title="Kirtans">kirtans</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tappa" title="Tappa">tappas</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Brahmo" title="Brahmo">Brahmo</a> hymnody was subdued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson192631_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson192631-46">[44]</a></sup> In 1880 he returned to Bengal degree-less, resolving to reconcile European novelty with Brahmo traditions, taking the best from each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b11â12_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b11â12-47">[45]</a></sup> After returning to Bengal, Tagore regularly published poems, stories, and novels. These had a profound impact within Bengal itself but received little national attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> In 1883 he married 10-year-old<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Selected_Letters_of_Rabindranath_Tagore._49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Selected_Letters_of_Rabindranath_Tagore.-49">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mrinalini_Devi" title="Mrinalini Devi">Mrinalini Devi</a>, born Bhabatarini, 1873â1902 (this was a common practice at the time). They had five children, two of whom died in childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995373_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995373-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="949" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg/220px-Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg/330px-Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg/440px-Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_family_boat_Padma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tagore family boat (bajra or <a href="/wiki/Budgerow" title="Budgerow">budgerow</a>), the "Padma".</div></div></div>
<p>In 1890 Tagore began managing his vast ancestral estates in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shelaidaha" title="Shelaidaha">Shelaidaha</a> (today a region of Bangladesh); he was joined there by his wife and children in 1898. Tagore released his <i>Manasi</i> poems (1890), among his best-known work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott200910_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott200910-51">[49]</a></sup> As <i><a href="/wiki/Zamindar" title="Zamindar">Zamindar</a> Babu</i>, Tagore criss-crossed the <a href="/wiki/Padma_River" title="Padma River">Padma River</a> in command of the <i>Padma</i>, the luxurious family barge (also known as "<a href="/wiki/Budgerow" title="Budgerow">budgerow</a>"). He collected mostly token rents and blessed villagers who in turn honoured him with banquetsâoccasionally of dried rice and sour milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995109â111_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995109â111-52">[50]</a></sup> He met <a href="/wiki/Gagan_Harkara" title="Gagan Harkara">Gagan Harkara</a>, through whom he became familiar with <a href="/wiki/Baul" title="Baul">Baul</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lalon_Shah" title="Lalon Shah">Lalon Shah</a>, whose folk songs greatly influenced Tagore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> Tagore worked to popularise Lalon's songs. The period 1891â1895, Tagore's <i>Sadhana</i> period, named after one of his magazines, was his most productive;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson192620_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson192620-28">[26]</a></sup> in these years he wrote more than half the stories of the three-volume, 84-story <i>Galpaguchchha</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196145_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196145-43">[41]</a></sup> Its ironic and grave tales examined the voluptuous poverty of an idealised rural Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995109_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995109-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Santiniketan:_1901.E2.80.931932"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Santiniketan:_1901â1932">Santiniketan: 1901â1932</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_years_of_Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Middle years of Rabindranath Tagore">Middle years of Rabindranath Tagore</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagore-THU.jpg"><img alt="Posed group black-and-white photograph of seven Chinese men, possibly academics, in formal wear: two wear European-style suits, the five others wear Chinese traditional dress; four of the seven sit on the floor in the foreground; another sits on a chair behind them at centre-left; two others stand in the background. They surround an eighth man who is robed, bearded, and sitting in a chair placed at centre-left. Four elegant windows are behind them in a line." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tagore-THU.jpg/275px-Tagore-THU.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tagore-THU.jpg/413px-Tagore-THU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Tagore-THU.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagore-THU.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tsinghua_University" title="Tsinghua University">Tsinghua University</a>, 1924</div></div></div>
<p>In 1901 Tagore moved to <a href="/wiki/Santiniketan" title="Santiniketan">Santiniketan</a> to found an <a href="/wiki/Ashram" title="Ashram">ashram</a> with a marble-floored prayer hallâ<i>The <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Mandir" title="wikt:Mandir">Mandir</a></i>âan experimental school, groves of trees, gardens, a library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995133_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995133-55">[53]</a></sup> There his wife and two of his children died. His father died in 1905. He received monthly payments as part of his inheritance and income from the Maharaja of <a href="/wiki/Tripura" title="Tripura">Tripura</a>, sales of his family's jewellery, his seaside bungalow in <a href="/wiki/Puri" title="Puri">Puri</a>, and a derisory 2,000 rupees in book royalties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995139â140_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995139â140-56">[54]</a></sup> He gained Bengali and foreign readers alike; he published <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naivedya" title="Naivedya">Naivedya</a></i> (1901) and <i>Kheya</i> (1906) and translated poems into free verse.
</p><p>In 1912, Tagore translated his 1910 work <i><a href="/wiki/Gitanjali" title="Gitanjali">Gitanjali</a></i> into English.  While on a trip to London, he shared these poems with admirers including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Butler_Yeats" title="William Butler Yeats">William Butler Yeats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>.  London's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/India_Society" title="India Society">India Society</a> published the work in a limited edition, and the American magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Poetry_(magazine)" title="Poetry (magazine)">Poetry</a></i> published a selection from <i>Gitanjali</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup>   In November 1913, Tagore learned he had won that year's <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Academy" title="Swedish Academy">Swedish Academy</a> appreciated the idealisticâand for Westernersâaccessible nature of a small body of his translated material focused on the 1912 <i>Gitanjali: Song Offerings</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHjÃ¤rne1913_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHjÃ¤rne1913-58">[56]</a></sup> He was awarded a knighthood by King George V in the <a href="/wiki/1915_Birthday_Honours" title="1915 Birthday Honours">1915 Birthday Honours</a>, but Tagore renounced it after the 1919 <a href="/wiki/Jallianwala_Bagh_massacre" title="Jallianwala Bagh massacre">Jallianwala Bagh massacre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> Renouncing the knighthood, Tagore wrote in a letter addressed to <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Thesiger,_1st_Viscount_Chelmsford" title="Frederic Thesiger, 1st Viscount Chelmsford">Lord Chelmsford</a>, the then British Viceroy of India, "The disproportionate severity of the punishments inflicted upon the unfortunate people and the methods of carrying them out, we are convinced, are without parallel in the history of civilised governments...The time has come when badges of honour make our shame glaring in their incongruous context of humiliation, and I for my part wish to stand, shorn of all special distinctions, by the side of my country men."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1919, he was invited by the president and chairman of Anjuman-e-Islamia, <a href="/wiki/Syed_Abdul_Majid" title="Syed Abdul Majid">Syed Abdul Majid</a> to visit <a href="/wiki/Sylhet" title="Sylhet">Sylhet</a> for the first time. The event attracted over 5000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-star_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-star-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1921, Tagore and agricultural economist <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Knight_Elmhirst" title="Leonard Knight Elmhirst">Leonard Elmhirst</a> set up the "Institute for Rural Reconstruction", later renamed Shriniketan or "Abode of Welfare", in <a href="/wiki/Surul" title="Surul">Surul</a>, a village near the <i>ashram</i>. With it, Tagore sought to moderate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi" title="Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi">Gandhi's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Swaraj" title="Swaraj">Swaraj</a></i> protests, which he occasionally blamed for British India's perceived mental â and thus ultimately colonial â decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995239â240_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995239â240-63">[61]</a></sup> He sought aid from donors, officials, and scholars worldwide to "free village[s] from the shackles of helplessness and ignorance" by "vitalis[ing] knowledge".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995242_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995242-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995308â309_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995308â309-65">[63]</a></sup> In the early 1930s he targeted ambient "abnormal caste consciousness" and <a href="/wiki/Untouchability" title="Untouchability">untouchability</a>. He lectured against these, he penned <a href="/wiki/Dalit" title="Dalit">Dalit</a> heroes for his poems and his dramas, and he campaignedâsuccessfullyâto open <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guruvayoor_Temple" title="Guruvayoor Temple">Guruvayoor Temple</a> to Dalits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995303_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995303-66">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995309_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995309-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Twilight_years:_1932.E2.80.931941"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Twilight_years:_1932â1941">Twilight years: 1932â1941</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:179px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643,_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="547" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643%2C_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/177px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643%2C_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643%2C_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/266px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643%2C_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643%2C_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/354px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643%2C_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg 2x" width="177"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-11643,_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Germany, 1931</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="690" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg/220px-Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg/330px-Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg/440px-Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Last_pic_of_Tagore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Last picture of Rabindranath, 1941</div></div></div>
<p>Dutta and Robinson describe this phase of Tagore's life as being one of a "peripatetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Litterateur" title="Litterateur">litterateur</a>". It affirmed his opinion that human divisions were shallow. During a May 1932 visit to a <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> encampment in the Iraqi desert, the tribal chief told him that "Our <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Prophet</a> has said that a true Muslim is he by whose words and deeds not the least of his brother-men may ever come to any harm ..." Tagore confided in his diary: "I was startled into recognizing in his words the voice of essential humanity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995317_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995317-68">[66]</a></sup> To the end Tagore scrutinised orthodoxyâand in 1934, he struck. That year, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1934_Nepal-Bihar_earthquake" title="1934 Nepal-Bihar earthquake">an earthquake</a> hit Bihar and killed thousands. Gandhi hailed it as seismic <i><a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a></i>, as divine retribution avenging the oppression of Dalits. Tagore rebuked him for his seemingly ignominious implications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995312â313_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995312â313-69">[67]</a></sup> He mourned the perennial poverty of Calcutta and the socioeconomic decline of Bengal, and detailed these newly plebeian aesthetics in an unrhymed hundred-line poem whose technique of searing double-vision foreshadowed <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a>'s film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apur_Sansar_(The_World_of_Apu)" title="Apur Sansar (The World of Apu)">Apur Sansar</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995335â338_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995335â338-70">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995342_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995342-71">[69]</a></sup> Fifteen new volumes appeared, among them prose-poem works <i>Punashcha</i> (1932), <i>Shes Saptak</i> (1935), and <i>Patraput</i> (1936). Experimentation continued in his prose-songs and dance-dramasâ <i><a href="/wiki/Chitra_(play)" title="Chitra (play)">Chitra</a></i> (1914), <i>Shyama</i> (1939), and <i>Chandalika</i> (1938)â and in his novelsâ <i>Dui Bon</i> (1933), <i>Malancha</i> (1934), and <i>Char Adhyay</i> (1934).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:20%; ; font-size: 85%;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Clouds come floating into my life, no longer to carry rain or usher storm, but to add color to my sunset sky.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Â âVerse 292, <i>Stray Birds</i>, 1916.</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Tagore's remit expanded to science in his last years, as hinted in <i>Visva-Parichay</i>, a 1937 collection of essays. His respect for scientific laws and his exploration of biology, physics, and astronomy informed his poetry, which exhibited extensive naturalism and verisimilitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200428_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200428-73">[71]</a></sup> He wove the <i>process</i> of science, the narratives of scientists, into stories in <i>Se</i> (1937), <i>Tin Sangi</i> (1940), and <i>Galpasalpa</i> (1941). His last five years were marked by chronic pain and two long periods of illness. These began when Tagore lost consciousness in late 1937; he remained comatose and near death for a time. This was followed in late 1940 by a similar spell, from which he never recovered. Poetry from these valetudinary years is among his finest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995338_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995338-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIndo-Asian_News_Service2005_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIndo-Asian_News_Service2005-75">[73]</a></sup> A period of prolonged agony ended with Tagore's death on 7 August 1941, aged 80.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobelfacts_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-nobelfacts-21">[20]</a></sup> He was in an upstairs room of the Jorasanko mansion in which he grew up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995367_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995367-76">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995363_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995363-77">[75]</a></sup> The date is still mourned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Daily_Star2009_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Daily_Star2009-78">[76]</a></sup> A. K. Sen, brother of the first chief election commissioner, received dictation from Tagore on 30 July 1941, a day prior to a scheduled operation: his last poem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESigi200689_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESigi200689-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I'm lost in the middle of my birthday. I want my friends, their touch, with the earth's last love. I will take life's final offering, I will take the human's last blessing. Today my sack is empty. I have given completely whatever I had to give. In return if I receive anythingâsome love, some forgivenessâthen I will take it with me when I step on the boat that crosses to the festival of the wordless end.</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Travels">Travels</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="606" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/220px-Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/330px-Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/440px-Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jawaharlal_Nehru_and_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a> and Rabindranath Tagore</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:20%; ; font-size: 85%;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Our passions and desires are unruly, but our character subdues these elements into a harmonious whole. Does something similar to this happen in the physical world? Are the elements rebellious, dynamic with individual impulse? And is there a principle in the physical world which dominates them and puts them into an orderly organization?
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">â Interviewed by Einstein, 14 April 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1930222â225_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1930222â225-80">[78]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="654" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg/220px-Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg/330px-Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg/440px-Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_with_Einstein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rabindranath with <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a> in 1930</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_Iran.jpg"><img alt="Group shot of dozens of people assembled at the entrance of an imposing building; two columns in view. All subjects face the camera. All but two are dressed in lounge suits: a woman at front-center wears light-coloured Persian garb; the man to her left, first row, wears a white beard and dark-coloured oriental cap and robes." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="302" data-file-width="432" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Tagore_Iran.jpg/220px-Tagore_Iran.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Tagore_Iran.jpg/330px-Tagore_Iran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/Tagore_Iran.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_Iran.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majlis_of_Iran" title="Majlis of Iran">Majlis</a> (Iranian parliament) in <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, 1932</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1878 and 1932, Tagore set foot in more than thirty countries on five continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995374â376_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995374â376-81">[79]</a></sup> In 1912, he took a sheaf of his translated works to England, where they gained attention from missionary and Gandhi protÃ©gÃ© <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_F._Andrews" title="Charles F. Andrews">Charles F. Andrews</a>, Irish poet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Butler_Yeats" title="William Butler Yeats">William Butler Yeats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bridges" title="Robert Bridges">Robert Bridges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rhys" title="Ernest Rhys">Ernest Rhys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sturge_Moore" title="Thomas Sturge Moore">Thomas Sturge Moore</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995178â179_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995178â179-82">[80]</a></sup> Yeats wrote the preface to the English translation of <i>Gitanjali</i>; Andrews joined Tagore at Santiniketan. In November 1912 Tagore began touring the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUniversity_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUniversity_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign-83">[81]</a></sup> and the United Kingdom, staying in <a href="/wiki/Butterton" title="Butterton">Butterton</a>, Staffordshire with Andrews's clergymen friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty19611â2_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty19611â2-84">[82]</a></sup> From May 1916 until April 1917, he lectured in Japan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995206_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995206-86">[84]</a></sup> He denounced nationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoganPandit200356â58_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoganPandit200356â58-87">[85]</a></sup> His essay "Nationalism in India" was scorned and praised; it was admired by <a href="/wiki/Romain_Rolland" title="Romain Rolland">Romain Rolland</a> and other pacifists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961182_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961182-88">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after returning home the 63-year-old Tagore accepted an invitation from the Peruvian government. He travelled to Mexico. Each government pledged <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$</span>100,000 to his school to commemorate the visits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995253_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995253-89">[87]</a></sup> A week after his 6 November 1924 arrival in Buenos Aires,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995256_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995256-90">[88]</a></sup> an ill Tagore shifted to the Villa MiralrÃ­o at the behest of <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Ocampo" title="Victoria Ocampo">Victoria Ocampo</a>. He left for home in January 1925. In May 1926 Tagore reached Naples; the next day he met <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Mussolini</a> in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995267_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995267-91">[89]</a></sup> Their warm rapport ended when Tagore pronounced upon <i>Il Duce</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s fascist finesse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995270â271_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995270â271-92">[90]</a></sup> He had earlier enthused: "[w]ithout any doubt he is a great personality. There is such a massive vigour in that head that it reminds one of Michael Angelo's chisel." A "fire-bath" of fascism was to have educed "the immortal soul of Italy ... clothed in quenchless light".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKundu2009_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKundu2009-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 November 1926 Tagore arrived to Hungary and spent some time on the shore of Lake Balaton in the city of BalatonfÃ¼red, recovering from heart problems at a sanitarium. He planted a tree and a bust statue was placed there in 1956 (a gift from the Indian government, the work of Rasithan Kashar, replaced by a newly gifted statue in 2005) and the lakeside promenade still bears his name since 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 July 1927 Tagore and two companions began a four-month tour of Southeast Asia. They visited Bali, Java, Kuala Lumpur, Malacca, Penang, Siam, and Singapore. The resultant travelogues compose <i>Jatri</i> (1929).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty19611_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty19611-95">[93]</a></sup> In early 1930 he left Bengal for a nearly year-long tour of Europe and the United States. Upon returning to Britainâand as his paintings were exhibited in Paris and Londonâhe lodged at a Birmingham Quaker settlement. He wrote his Oxford <a href="/wiki/Hibbert_Lectures" title="Hibbert Lectures">Hibbert Lectures</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[c]</a></sup> and spoke at the annual London Quaker meet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995289â292_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995289â292-97">[94]</a></sup> There, addressing relations between the British and the Indians â a topic he would tackle repeatedly over the next two years â Tagore spoke of a "dark chasm of aloofness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995303â304_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995303â304-98">[95]</a></sup> He visited <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan_III" title="Aga Khan III">Aga Khan III</a>, stayed at <a href="/wiki/Dartington_Hall" title="Dartington Hall">Dartington Hall</a>, toured Denmark, Switzerland, and Germany from June to mid-September 1930, then went on into the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995292â293_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995292â293-99">[96]</a></sup> In April 1932 Tagore, intrigued by the Persian mystic <a href="/wiki/Hafez" title="Hafez">Hafez</a>, was hosted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reza_Shah_Pahlavi" title="Reza Shah Pahlavi">Reza Shah Pahlavi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty19612_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty19612-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995315_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995315-101">[98]</a></sup> In his other travels, Tagore interacted with <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Frost" title="Robert Frost">Robert Frost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.G._Wells" title="H.G. Wells">H.G. Wells</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romain_Rolland" title="Romain Rolland">Romain Rolland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196199_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196199-102">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961100â103_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961100â103-103">[100]</a></sup> Visits to Persia and Iraq (in 1932) and Sri Lanka (in 1933) composed Tagore's final foreign tour, and his dislike of <a href="/wiki/Communalism_(South_Asia)" title="Communalism (South Asia)">communalism</a> and nationalism only deepened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995317_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995317-68">[66]</a></sup>
Vice-President of India M. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamid_Ansari" title="Hamid Ansari">Hamid Ansari</a> has said that Rabindranath Tagore heralded the cultural rapprochement between communities, societies and nations much before it became the liberal norm of conduct.
Tagore was a man ahead of his time. He wrote in 1932, while on a visit to Iran, that "each country of Asia will solve its own historical problems according to its strength, nature and needs, but the lamp they will each carry on their path to progress will converge to illuminate the common ray of knowledge."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Works_of_Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Works of Rabindranath Tagore">Works of Rabindranath Tagore</a></div>
<p>Known mostly for his poetry, Tagore wrote novels, essays, short stories, travelogues, dramas, and thousands of songs. Of Tagore's prose, his short stories are perhaps most highly regarded; he is indeed credited with originating the Bengali-language version of the genre. His works are frequently noted for their rhythmic, optimistic, and lyrical nature. Such stories mostly borrow from the lives of common people. Tagore's non-fiction grappled with history, linguistics, and spirituality. He wrote autobiographies. His travelogues, essays, and lectures were compiled into several volumes, including <i>Europe Jatrir Patro</i> (<i>Letters from Europe</i>) and <i>Manusher Dhormo</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Religion_of_Man" title="The Religion of Man">The Religion of Man</a></i>). His brief chat with <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>, "Note on the Nature of Reality", is included as an appendix to the latter. On the occasion of Tagore's 150th birthday, an anthology (titled <i>Kalanukromik Rabindra Rachanabali</i>) of the total body of his works is currently being published in Bengali in chronological order. This includes all versions of each work and fills about eighty volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPandey2011_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPandey2011-105">[102]</a></sup> In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Press" title="Harvard University Press">Harvard University Press</a> collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Visva-Bharati_University" title="Visva-Bharati University">Visva-Bharati University</a> to publish <i><a href="/wiki/The_Essential_Tagore" title="The Essential Tagore">The Essential Tagore</a></i>, the largest anthology of Tagore's works available in English; it was edited by <a href="/wiki/Fakrul_Alam" title="Fakrul Alam">Fakrul Alam</a> and Radha Chakravarthy and marks the 150th anniversary of Tagore's birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drama">Drama</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="943" data-file-width="1440" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/220px-Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/330px-Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg/440px-Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Valmiki_Pratibha_Indira_Devi_%26_Rabindranath_Tagore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tagore performing the title role in <i>Valmiki Pratibha</i> (1881) with his niece <a href="/wiki/Indira_Devi_Chaudhurani" title="Indira Devi Chaudhurani">Indira Devi</a> as the goddess <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Tagore's experiences with drama began when he was sixteen, with his brother <a href="/wiki/Jyotirindranath_Tagore" title="Jyotirindranath Tagore">Jyotirindranath</a>. He wrote his first original dramatic piece when he was twenty â <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valmiki_Pratibha" title="Valmiki Pratibha">Valmiki Pratibha</a></i> which was shown at the Tagore's mansion. Tagore stated that his works sought to articulate "the play of feeling and not of action". In 1890 he wrote <i>Visarjan</i> (an adaptation of his novella <i>Rajarshi</i>), which has been regarded as his finest drama. In the original Bengali language, such works included intricate subplots and extended monologues. Later, Tagore's dramas used more philosophical and allegorical themes. The play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Post_Office_(play)" title="The Post Office (play)">Dak Ghar</a></i> (<i>The Post Office'</i>; 1912), describes the child Amal defying his stuffy and puerile confines by ultimately "fall[ing] asleep", hinting his physical death. A story with borderless appealâgleaning rave reviews in Europeâ<i>Dak Ghar</i> dealt with death as, in Tagore's words, "spiritual freedom" from "the world of hoarded wealth and certified creeds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b21â22_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b21â22-107">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961123â124_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961123â124-108">[105]</a></sup> Another is Tagore's <i>Chandalika</i> (<i>Untouchable Girl</i>), which was modelled on an ancient Buddhist legend describing how <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ananda" title="Ananda">Ananda</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Gautama Buddha</a>'s disciple, asks a <a href="/wiki/Adivasi" title="Adivasi">tribal</a> girl for water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961124_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961124-109">[106]</a></sup> In <i>Raktakarabi</i> ("Red" or "Blood Oleanders") is an allegorical struggle against a kleptocrat king who rules over the residents of <i><a href="/wiki/Yaksha" title="Yaksha">Yaksha</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pur_(Vedic)" title="Pur (Vedic)">puri</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERay2007147â148_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERay2007147â148-110">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Chitrangada</i>, <i>Chandalika</i>, and <i>Shyama</i> are other key plays that have dance-drama adaptations, which together are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Rabindra_Nritya_Natya" title="Rabindra Nritya Natya">Rabindra Nritya Natya</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Short_stories">Short stories</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sabujpatra_logo_(1914-1927).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sabujpatra_logo_%281914-1927%29.jpg/170px-Sabujpatra_logo_%281914-1927%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sabujpatra_logo_%281914-1927%29.jpg/255px-Sabujpatra_logo_%281914-1927%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Sabujpatra_logo_%281914-1927%29.jpg/340px-Sabujpatra_logo_%281914-1927%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sabujpatra_logo_(1914-1927).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabuj_Patra" title="Sabuj Patra">Sabuj Patra</a></i> magazine, edited by <a href="/wiki/Pramatha_Chaudhuri" title="Pramatha Chaudhuri">Pramatha Chaudhuri</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tagore began his career in short stories in 1877âwhen he was only sixteenâwith "Bhikharini" ("The Beggar Woman").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chakravarty_1961_45_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chakravarty_1961_45-111">[108]</a></sup> With this, Tagore effectively invented the Bengali-language short story genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1997265_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1997265-112">[109]</a></sup> The four years from 1891 to 1895 are known as Tagore's "Sadhana" period (named for one of Tagore's magazines). This period was among Tagore's most fecund, yielding more than half the stories contained in the three-volume <i>Galpaguchchha</i>, which itself is a collection of eighty-four stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chakravarty_1961_45_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chakravarty_1961_45-111">[108]</a></sup> Such stories usually showcase Tagore's reflections upon his surroundings, on modern and fashionable ideas, and on interesting mind puzzles (which Tagore was fond of testing his intellect with). Tagore typically associated his earliest stories (such as those of the "<i>Sadhana</i>" period) with an exuberance of vitality and spontaneity; these characteristics were intimately connected with Tagore's life in the common villages of, among others, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patisar" title="Patisar">Patisar</a>, Shajadpur, and <a href="/wiki/Shilaidaha" title="Shilaidaha">Shilaida</a> while managing the Tagore family's vast landholdings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chakravarty_1961_45_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chakravarty_1961_45-111">[108]</a></sup> There, he beheld the lives of India's poor and common people; Tagore thereby took to examining their lives with a penetrative depth and feeling that was singular in Indian literature up to that point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chakravarty_1961_45-46_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chakravarty_1961_45-46-113">[110]</a></sup> In particular, such stories as "<a href="/wiki/Kabuliwala_(short_story)" title="Kabuliwala (short story)">Kabuliwala</a>" ("The Fruitseller from <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a>", published in 1892), "Kshudita Pashan" ("The Hungry Stones") (August 1895), and "Atithi" ("The Runaway", 1895) typified this analytic focus on the downtrodden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chakravarty_1961_46_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chakravarty_1961_46-114">[111]</a></sup> Many of the other <i>Galpaguchchha</i> stories were written in Tagore's <i>Sabuj Patra</i> period from 1914 to 1917, also named after one of the magazines that Tagore edited and heavily contributed to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chakravarty_1961_45_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chakravarty_1961_45-111">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Novels">Novels</span></h3>
<p>Tagore wrote eight novels and four novellas, among them <i>Chaturanga</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shesher_Kobita" title="Shesher Kobita">Shesher Kobita</a></i>, <i>Char Odhay</i>, and <i>Noukadubi</i>. <i>Ghare Baire</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Home_and_the_World" title="The Home and the World">The Home and the World</a></i>)âthrough the lens of the idealistic <i><a href="/wiki/Zamindar" title="Zamindar">zamindar</a></i> protagonist Nikhilâexcoriates rising Indian nationalism, terrorism, and religious zeal in the <a href="/wiki/Swadeshi_movement" title="Swadeshi movement"><i>Swadeshi</i> movement</a>; a frank expression of Tagore's conflicted sentiments, it emerged from a 1914 bout of depression. The novel ends in Hindu-Muslim violence and Nikhil'sâlikely mortalâwounding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995192â194_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995192â194-115">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Gora</i> raises controversial questions regarding the Indian identity. As with <i>Ghare Baire</i>, matters of self-identity (<i><a href="/wiki/J%C4%81ti" title="JÄti">jÄti</a></i>), personal freedom, and religion are developed in the context of a family story and love triangle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995154â155_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995154â155-116">[113]</a></sup> In it an Irish boy orphaned in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sepoy_Mutiny" title="Sepoy Mutiny">Sepoy Mutiny</a> is raised by Hindus as the titular <i>gora</i>â"whitey". Ignorant of his foreign origins, he chastises Hindu religious backsliders out of love for the indigenous Indians and solidarity with them against his hegemon-compatriots. He falls for a Brahmo girl, compelling his worried foster father to reveal his lost past and cease his nativist zeal. As a "true dialectic" advancing "arguments for and against strict traditionalism", it tackles the colonial conundrum by "portray[ing] the value of all positions within a particular frameÂ [...] not only syncretism, not only liberal orthodoxy, but the extremest reactionary traditionalism he defends by an appeal to what humans share." Among these Tagore highlights "identityÂ [...] conceived of as <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">dharma</a>.</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHogan2000213â214_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHogan2000213â214-117">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Jogajog" title="Jogajog">Jogajog</a></i> (<i>Relationships</i>), the heroine Kumudiniâbound by the ideals of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%9Aiva" title="Åiva">Åiva</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dakshayani" title="Dakshayani">Sati</a></i>, exemplified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dakshayani" title="Dakshayani">DÄkshÄyani</a>âis torn between her pity for the sinking fortunes of her progressive and compassionate elder brother and his foil: her roue of a husband. Tagore flaunts his feminist leanings; <i>pathos</i> depicts the plight and ultimate demise of women trapped by pregnancy, duty, and family honour; he simultaneously trucks with Bengal's putrescent landed gentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMukherjee2004_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMukherjee2004-118">[115]</a></sup> The story revolves around the underlying rivalry between two familiesâthe Chatterjees, aristocrats now on the decline (Biprodas) and the Ghosals (Madhusudan), representing new money and new arrogance. Kumudini, Biprodas' sister, is caught between the two as she is married off to Madhusudan. She had risen in an observant and sheltered traditional home, as had all her female relations.
</p><p>Others were uplifting: <i>Shesher Kobita</i>âtranslated twice as <i>Last Poem</i> and <i>Farewell Song</i>âis his most lyrical novel, with poems and rhythmic passages written by a poet protagonist. It contains elements of satire and postmodernism and has stock characters who gleefully attack the reputation of an old, outmoded, oppressively renowned poet who, incidentally, goes by a familiar name: "Rabindranath Tagore". Though his novels remain among the least-appreciated of his works, they have been given renewed attention via film adaptations by Ray and others: <i><a href="/wiki/Chokher_Bali_(film)" title="Chokher Bali (film)">Chokher Bali</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ghare_Baire_(film)" title="Ghare Baire (film)">Ghare Baire</a></i> are exemplary. In the first, Tagore inscribes Bengali society via its heroine: a rebellious widow who would live for herself alone. He pillories the custom of perpetual mourning on the part of widows, who were not allowed to remarry, who were consigned to seclusion and loneliness. Tagore wrote of it: "I have always regretted the ending".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">Poetry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:167px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1359" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg/165px-Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg/248px-Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg/330px-Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg 2x" width="165"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gitanjali_11_title_page.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of the 1913 <a href="/wiki/Macmillan_Publishers" title="Macmillan Publishers">Macmillan</a> edition of Tagore's <i>Gitanjali</i>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg"><img alt='Three-verse handwritten composition; each verse has original Bengali with English-language translation below: "My fancies are fireflies: specks of living light twinkling in the dark. The same voice murmurs in these desultory lines, which is born in wayside pansies letting hasty glances pass by. The butterfly does not count years but moments, and therefore has enough time."' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="669" data-file-width="354" decoding="async" height="321" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg/170px-Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg/255px-Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg/340px-Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_handwriting_Bengali.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of a poem written by Tagore in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, 1926.</div></div></div>
<p>Internationally, <i>Gitanjali</i> (<a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>: <span lang="bn">à¦à§à¦¤à¦¾à¦à§à¦à¦²à¦¿</span>) is Tagore's best-known collection of poetry, for which he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> in 1913. Tagore was the first non-European to receive a Nobel Prize in Literature and second non-European to receive a Nobel Prize after <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides <i>Gitanjali</i>, other notable works include <i>Manasi</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sonar_Tori" title="Sonar Tori">Sonar Tori</a></i> ("Golden Boat"), <i>Balaka</i> ("Wild Geese" â the title being a metaphor for migrating souls)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson19951_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson19951-120">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tagore's poetic style, which proceeds from a lineage established by 15th- and 16th-century Vaishnava poets, ranges from classical formalism to the comic, visionary, and ecstatic. He was influenced by the atavistic mysticism of <a href="/wiki/Vyasa" title="Vyasa">Vyasa</a> and other <i>rishi</i>-authors of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upanishad" title="Upanishad">Upanishads</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bhakti" title="Bhakti">Bhakti</a>-<a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> mystic <a href="/wiki/Kabir" title="Kabir">Kabir</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ramprasad_Sen" title="Ramprasad Sen">Ramprasad Sen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoy1977201_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoy1977201-121">[118]</a></sup> Tagore's most innovative and mature poetry embodies his exposure to Bengali rural folk music, which included mystic <a href="/wiki/Baul" title="Baul">Baul</a> ballads such as those of the bard <a href="/wiki/Lalon" title="Lalon">Lalon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell200394_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell200394-122">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrban200118_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrban200118-123">[120]</a></sup> These, rediscovered and repopularised by Tagore, resemble 19th-century KartÄbhajÄ hymns that emphasise inward divinity and rebellion against bourgeois <i>bhadralok</i> religious and social orthodoxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrban20016â7_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrban20016â7-124">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrban200116_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrban200116-125">[122]</a></sup> During his Shelaidaha years, his poems took on a lyrical voice of the <i>moner manush</i>, the BÄuls' "man within the heart" and Tagore's "life force of his deep recesses", or meditating upon the <i>jeevan devata</i>âthe demiurge or the "living God within".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011-20">[19]</a></sup> This figure connected with divinity through appeal to nature and the emotional interplay of human drama. Such tools saw use in his BhÄnusiá¹ha poems chronicling the <a href="/wiki/Radha" title="Radha">Radha</a>-<a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> romance, which were repeatedly revised over the course of seventy years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell200395_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell200395-126">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell20037_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreStewartTwichell20037-127">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later, with the development of new poetic ideas in Bengal â many originating from younger poets seeking to break with Tagore's style â Tagore absorbed new poetic concepts, which allowed him to further develop a unique identity. Examples of this include <i>Africa</i> and <i>Camalia</i>, which are among the better known of his latter poems.
</p>
<h3><span id="Songs_.28Rabindra_Sangeet.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Songs_(Rabindra_Sangeet)">Songs (Rabindra Sangeet)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Draft:Rabindranath_Tagore_discography" title="Draft:Rabindranath Tagore discography">Draft:Rabindranath Tagore discography</a></div>
<p>Tagore was a prolific composer with around 2,230 songs to his credit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DasguptaGuha2013_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-DasguptaGuha2013-128">[125]</a></sup> His songs are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Rabindra_Sangeet" title="Rabindra Sangeet">rabindrasangit</a></i> ("Tagore Song"), which merges fluidly into his literature, most of whichâpoems or parts of novels, stories, or plays alikeâwere lyricised. Influenced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Thumri" title="Thumri">thumri</a></i> style of <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_classical_music" title="Hindustani classical music">Hindustani music</a>, they ran the entire gamut of human emotion, ranging from his early dirge-like Brahmo devotional hymns to quasi-erotic compositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199594_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199594-129">[126]</a></sup> They emulated the tonal colour of classical <i><a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">ragas</a></i> to varying extents. Some songs mimicked a given raga's melody and rhythm faithfully; others newly blended elements of different <i>ragas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDasgupta2001_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDasgupta2001-130">[127]</a></sup> Yet about nine-tenths of his work was not <i>bhanga gaan</i>, the body of tunes revamped with "fresh value" from select Western, Hindustani, Bengali folk and other regional flavours "external" to Tagore's own ancestral culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="39" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Tagore_singing_Jana_Gana_Mana.webm" height="141" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Tagore_singing_Jana_Gana_Mana.webm/250px--Tagore_singing_Jana_Gana_Mana.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="250"><source data-bandwidth="229752" data-framerate="25" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f8/Tagore_singing_Jana_Gana_Mana.webm/Tagore_singing_Jana_Gana_Mana.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
