codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagore_singing_Jana_Gana_Mana.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rabindranath Tagore reciting Jana Gana Mana</div></div></div>
<p>In 1971, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amar_Shonar_Bangla" title="Amar Shonar Bangla">Amar Shonar Bangla</a></i> became the national anthem of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>. It was written â ironically â to protest the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1905_Partition_of_Bengal" title="1905 Partition of Bengal">1905 Partition of Bengal</a> along communal lines: cutting off the Muslim-majority East Bengal from Hindu-dominated West Bengal was to avert a regional bloodbath. Tagore saw the partition as a cunning plan to stop the <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">independence movement</a>, and he aimed to rekindle Bengali unity and tar communalism. <i><a href="/wiki/Jana_Gana_Mana" title="Jana Gana Mana">Jana Gana Mana</a></i> was written in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shadhu-bhasha" title="Shadhu-bhasha">shadhu-bhasha</a></i>, a Sanskritised form of Bengali,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup> and is the first of five stanzas of the Brahmo hymn <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bharot_Bhagyo_Bidhata" title="Bharot Bhagyo Bidhata">Bharot Bhagyo Bidhata</a></i> that Tagore composed. It was first sung in 1911 at a Calcutta session of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress" title="Indian National Congress">Indian National Congress</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup> and was adopted in 1950 by the Constituent Assembly of the Republic of India as its national anthem.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka_Matha" title="Sri Lanka Matha">Sri Lanka's National Anthem</a> was inspired by his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RB1_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-RB1-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>For Bengalis, the songs' appeal, stemming from the combination of emotive strength and beauty described as surpassing even Tagore's poetry, was such that the <i>Modern Review</i> observed that "[t]here is in Bengal no cultured home where Rabindranath's songs are not sung or at least attempted to be sung... Even illiterate villagers sing his songs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995359_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995359-133">[130]</a></sup> Tagore influenced <i>sitar</i> maestro <a href="/wiki/Vilayat_Khan" title="Vilayat Khan">Vilayat Khan</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sarod" title="Sarod">sarodiyas</a></i> Buddhadev Dasgupta and <a href="/wiki/Amjad_Ali_Khan" title="Amjad Ali Khan">Amjad Ali Khan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDasgupta2001_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDasgupta2001-130">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_works">Art works</span></h3>

1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindra_Bhavana_collection_2155_pastel_mask.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Primitivism: a pastel-coloured rendition of a <a href="/wiki/Malagan" title="Malagan">Malagan</a> mask from northern <a href="/wiki/New_Ireland_Province" title="New Ireland Province">New Ireland</a>, Papua New Guinea.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:156px;max-width:156px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_Tagore_Ra-Tha_seal_initials.jpg"><img alt='Black-and-white close-up photograph of a piece of wood boldly painted in unmixed solid strokes of black and white in a stylised semblance to "ro" and "tho" from the Bengali syllabary.' data-file-height="381" data-file-width="236" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rabindranath_Tagore_Ra-Tha_seal_initials.jpg/154px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Ra-Tha_seal_initials.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rabindranath_Tagore_Ra-Tha_seal_initials.jpg/231px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Ra-Tha_seal_initials.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Rabindranath_Tagore_Ra-Tha_seal_initials.jpg 2x" width="154"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Tagore's Bengali-language initials are worked into this "Ro-Tho" (of RAbindranath THAkur) wooden seal, stylistically similar to designs used in traditional <a href="/wiki/Haida_argillite_carvings" title="Haida argillite carvings">Haida carvings</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> region of North America. Tagore often embellished his manuscripts with such art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson2001_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson2001-134">[131]</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>At sixty, Tagore took up drawing and painting; successful exhibitions of his many worksâwhich made a debut appearance in Paris upon encouragement by artists he met in the south of France<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b222_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b222-135">[132]</a></sup>âwere held throughout Europe. He was likely red-green <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protanopia" title="Protanopia">colour blind</a>, resulting in works that exhibited strange colour schemes and off-beat aesthetics. Tagore was influenced by numerous styles, including <a href="/wiki/Scrimshaw" title="Scrimshaw">scrimshaw</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malanggan" title="Malanggan">Malanggan</a> people of northern <a href="/wiki/New_Ireland_(island)" title="New Ireland (island)">New Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haida_argillite_carvings" title="Haida argillite carvings">Haida carvings</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> region of North America, and woodcuts by the German <a href="/wiki/Max_Pechstein" title="Max Pechstein">Max Pechstein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDyson2001_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson2001-134">[131]</a></sup> His artist's eye for handwriting was revealed in the simple artistic and rhythmic <a href="/wiki/Leitmotif" title="Leitmotif">leitmotifs</a> embellishing the scribbles, cross-outs, and word layouts of his manuscripts. Some of Tagore's lyrics corresponded in a synesthetic sense with particular paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGhosh2011-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Surrounded by several painters Rabindranath had always wanted to paint. Writing and music, playwriting and acting came to him naturally and almost without training, as it did to several others in his family, and in even greater measure. But painting eluded him. Yet he tried repeatedly to master the art and there are several references to this in his early letters and reminiscence. In 1900 for instance, when he was nearing forty and already a celebrated writer, he wrote to Jagadishchandra Bose, "You will be surprised to hear that I am sitting with a sketchbook drawing. Needless to say, the pictures are not intended for any salon in Paris, they cause me not the least suspicion that the national gallery of any country will suddenly decide to raise taxes to acquire them. But, just as a mother lavishes most affection on her ugliest son, so I feel secretly drawn to the very skill that comes to me least easily." He also realized that he was using the eraser more than the pencil, and dissatisfied with the results he finally withdrew, deciding it was not for him to become a painter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>India's <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Modern_Art" title="National Gallery of Modern Art">National Gallery of Modern Art</a> lists 102 works by Tagore in its collections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_views_of_Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Political views of Rabindranath Tagore">Political views of Rabindranath Tagore</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg"><img alt="Photo of a formal function, an aged bald man and old woman in simple white robes are seated side-by-side with legs folded on a rug-strewn dais; the man looks at a bearded and garlanded old man seated on another dais at left. In the foreground, various ceremonial objects are arrayed; in the background, dozens of other people observe." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="455" data-file-width="795" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg/220px-Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg/330px-Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg/440px-Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhi-Tagore-cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tagore hosts Gandhi and wife Kasturba at Santiniketan in 1940</div></div></div>
<p>Tagore opposed <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> and supported Indian nationalists,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b127_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b127-139">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b210_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b210-140">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995304_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995304-141">[138]</a></sup> and these views were first revealed in <i>Manast</i>, which was mostly composed in his twenties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott200910_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott200910-51">[49]</a></sup> Evidence produced during the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93German_Conspiracy_Trial" title="HinduâGerman Conspiracy Trial">HinduâGerman Conspiracy Trial</a> and latter accounts affirm his awareness of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu%E2%80%93German_Conspiracy" title="HinduâGerman Conspiracy">Ghadarites</a>, and stated that he sought the support of Japanese Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Terauchi_Masatake" title="Terauchi Masatake">Terauchi Masatake</a> and former Premier <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Ckuma_Shigenobu" title="Åkuma Shigenobu">Åkuma Shigenobu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1948306_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1948306-142">[139]</a></sup> Yet he lampooned the <a href="/wiki/Swadeshi_movement" title="Swadeshi movement">Swadeshi movement</a>; he rebuked it in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cult_of_the_Charkha" title="The Cult of the Charkha">The Cult of the Charkha</a></i>, an acrid 1925 essay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995261_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995261-143">[140]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a>, Tagore rebelled against strongly nationalist forms of the independence movement, and he wanted to assert India's right to be independent without denying the importance of what India could learn from abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-144">[141]</a></sup> He urged the masses to avoid victimology and instead seek self-help and education, and he saw the presence of British administration as a "political symptom of our social disease". He maintained that, even for those at the extremes of poverty, "there can be no question of blind revolution"; preferable to it was a "steady and purposeful education".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b239â240_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b239â240-145">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961181_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty1961181-146">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:20%; ; font-size: 85%;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>So I repeat we never can have a true view of man unless we have a love for him. Civilisation must be judged and prized, not by the amount of power it has developed, but by how much it has evolved and given expression to, by its laws and institutions, the love of humanity.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">â <i>SÄdhanÄ: The Realisation of Life</i>, 1916.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1916111_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1916111-147">[144]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Such views enraged many. He escaped assassinationâand only narrowlyâby Indian expatriates during his stay in a San Francisco hotel in late 1916; the plot failed when his would-be assassins fell into argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995204_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995204-148">[145]</a></sup> Tagore wrote songs lionising the Indian independence movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995215â216_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995215â216-149">[146]</a></sup> Two of Tagore's more politically charged compositions, "<a href="/wiki/Chitto_Jetha_Bhayshunyo" title="Chitto Jetha Bhayshunyo">Chitto Jetha Bhayshunyo</a>" ("Where the Mind is Without Fear") and "<a href="/wiki/Ekla_Chalo_Re" title="Ekla Chalo Re">Ekla Chalo Re</a>" ("If They Answer Not to Thy Call, Walk Alone"), gained mass appeal, with the latter favoured by Gandhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChakrabortyBhattacharya2001157_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChakrabortyBhattacharya2001157-150">[147]</a></sup> Though somewhat critical of Gandhian activism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMehta1999_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMehta1999-151">[148]</a></sup> Tagore was key in resolving a Gandhiâ<a href="/wiki/B._R._Ambedkar" title="B. R. Ambedkar">Ambedkar</a> dispute involving separate electorates for untouchables, thereby mooting at least one of Gandhi's fasts "unto death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995306â307_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995306â307-152">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995339_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995339-153">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Repudiation_of_knighthood">Repudiation of knighthood</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_who_have_declined_a_British_honour#Renouncing_an_honour" title="List of people who have declined a British honour">List of people who have declined a British honour Â§Â Renouncing an honour</a></div><p>
Tagore renounced his knighthood in response to the <a href="/wiki/Jallianwala_Bagh_massacre" title="Jallianwala Bagh massacre">Jallianwala Bagh massacre</a> in 1919. In the repudiation letter to the Viceroy, <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Thesiger,_1st_Viscount_Chelmsford" title="Frederic Thesiger, 1st Viscount Chelmsford">Lord Chelmsford</a>, he wrote<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TOI2011-04_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-TOI2011-04-154">[151]</a></sup> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The time has come when badges of honour make our shame glaring in the incongruous context of humiliation, and I for my part, wish to stand, shorn, of all special distinctions, by the side of those of my countrymen who, for their so called insignificance, are liable to suffer degradation not fit for human beings.</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Santiniketan_and_Visva-Bharati">Santiniketan and Visva-Bharati</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kala_Bhavan,_Santiniketan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="508" data-file-width="613" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kala_Bhavan%2C_Santiniketan.jpg/220px-Kala_Bhavan%2C_Santiniketan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kala_Bhavan%2C_Santiniketan.jpg/330px-Kala_Bhavan%2C_Santiniketan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Kala_Bhavan%2C_Santiniketan.jpg/440px-Kala_Bhavan%2C_Santiniketan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kala_Bhavan,_Santiniketan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kala_Bhavan" title="Kala Bhavan">Kala Bhavan</a> (Institute of Fine Arts), <a href="/wiki/Santiniketan" title="Santiniketan">Santiniketan</a>, India</div></div></div>
<p>Tagore despised rote classroom schooling: in "The Parrot's Training", a bird is caged and force-fed textbook pagesâto death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b267_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b267-155">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagorePal2004_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagorePal2004-156">[153]</a></sup> Visiting Santa Barbara in 1917, Tagore conceived a new type of university: he sought to "make Santiniketan the connecting thread between India and the world [and] a world center for the study of humanity somewhere beyond the limits of nation and geography."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995204_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995204-148">[145]</a></sup> The school, which he named <a href="/wiki/Visva-Bharati_University" title="Visva-Bharati University">Visva-Bharati</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[d]</a></sup> had its foundation stone laid on 24 December 1918 and was inaugurated precisely three years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995220_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995220-157">[154]</a></sup> Tagore employed a <i><a href="/wiki/Brahmacharya" title="Brahmacharya">brahmacharya</a></i> system: <i>gurus</i> gave pupils personal guidanceâemotional, intellectual, and spiritual. Teaching was often done under trees. He staffed the school, he contributed his Nobel Prize monies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoy1977175_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoy1977175-159">[155]</a></sup> and his duties as steward-mentor at Santiniketan kept him busy: mornings he taught classes; afternoons and evenings he wrote the students' textbooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196127_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreChakravarty196127-160">[156]</a></sup> He fundraised widely for the school in Europe and the United States between 1919 and 1921.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995221_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995221-161">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theft_of_Nobel_Prize">Theft of Nobel Prize</span></h3>
<p>On 25 March 2004, Tagore's Nobel Prize was stolen from the safety vault of the Visva-Bharati University, along with several other of his belongings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[158]</a></sup> On 7 December 2004, the Swedish Academy decided to present two replicas of Tagore's Nobel Prize, one made of gold and the other made of bronze, to the Visva-Bharati University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[159]</a></sup> It inspired the fictional film <i><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Chor" title="Nobel Chor">Nobel Chor</a></i>. In 2016 a baul singer named Pradip Bauri, accused of sheltering the thieves, was arrested and the prize was returned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_and_legacy">Impact and legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Rabindranath_Tagore" title="List of things named after Rabindranath Tagore">List of things named after Rabindranath Tagore</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg/170px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg/255px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg/340px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_Tagore_Bust_in_Gordon_Square.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Tagore in <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Square" title="Gordon Square">Gordon Square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloomsbury" title="Bloomsbury">Bloomsbury</a>, London</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park,_Dublin,_Ireland.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3936" data-file-width="2624" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park%2C_Dublin%2C_Ireland.JPG/170px-Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park%2C_Dublin%2C_Ireland.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park%2C_Dublin%2C_Ireland.JPG/255px-Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park%2C_Dublin%2C_Ireland.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park%2C_Dublin%2C_Ireland.JPG/340px-Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park%2C_Dublin%2C_Ireland.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_Tagore%27s_bust_at_St_Stephen_Green_Park,_Dublin,_Ireland.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rabindranath Tagore's bust at St Stephen Green Park, <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, Ireland</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2944" data-file-width="4524" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg/220px-Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg/330px-Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg/440px-Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Samadhi_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_Arnab_Dutta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rabindranath Tagore Memorial, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nimtala_crematorium" title="Nimtala crematorium">Nimtala crematorium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hungary,_Balatonf%C3%BCred,_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Hungary%2C_Balatonf%C3%BCred%2C_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg/170px-Hungary%2C_Balatonf%C3%BCred%2C_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Hungary%2C_Balatonf%C3%BCred%2C_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg/255px-Hungary%2C_Balatonf%C3%BCred%2C_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Hungary%2C_Balatonf%C3%BCred%2C_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg/340px-Hungary%2C_Balatonf%C3%BCred%2C_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hungary,_Balatonf%C3%BCred,_Rabindranath_promenade_in_autumn_-_Tagore_statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Rabindranath in Tagore promenade, <a href="/wiki/Balatonf%C3%BCred" title="BalatonfÃ¼red">BalatonfÃ¼red</a>, Hungary</div></div></div>
<p>Every year, many events pay tribute to Tagore: <i>Kabipranam</i>, his birth anniversary, is celebrated by groups scattered across the globe; the annual Tagore Festival held in Urbana, Illinois (USA); <i>Rabindra Path Parikrama</i> walking pilgrimages from Kolkata to Santiniketan; and recitals of his poetry, which are held on important anniversaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUniversity_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUniversity_of_Illinois_at_Urbana-Champaign-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChakrabarti2001-166">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHatcher2001_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHatcher2001-167">[163]</a></sup> Bengali culture is fraught with this legacy: from language and arts to history and politics. Amartya Sen deemed Tagore a "towering figure", a "deeply relevant and many-sided contemporary thinker".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHatcher2001_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHatcher2001-167">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-144">[141]</a></sup> Tagore's Bengali originalsâthe 1939 <i>RabÄ«ndra RachanÄvalÄ«</i>âis canonised as one of his nation's greatest cultural treasures, and he was roped into a reasonably humble role: "the greatest poet India has produced".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKÃ¤mpchen2003_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKÃ¤mpchen2003-168">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg/150px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg/225px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg/300px-Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rabindranath_Tagore_Rabindranath_1861-1941_Indian_Poet_stayed_here_in_1912.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">Blue plaque</a> in honor of Tagore, erected in 1961 by London County Council at 3 Villas on the Heath, Vale of Health, Hampstead, London NW3 1BA, London Borough of Camden.</div></div></div>
<p>Tagore was renowned throughout much of Europe, North America, and East Asia. He co-founded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dartington_Hall_School" title="Dartington Hall School">Dartington Hall School</a>, a progressive coeducational institution;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarrell2000162_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarrell2000162-169">[165]</a></sup> in Japan, he influenced such figures as Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata">Yasunari Kawabata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995202_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995202-170">[166]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">colonial Vietnam</a> Tagore was a guide for the restless spirit of the radical writer and publicist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nguyen_An_Ninh" title="Nguyen An Ninh">Nguyen An Ninh</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[167]</a></sup> Tagore's works were widely translated into English, Dutch, German, Spanish, and other European languages by Czech Indologist <a href="/wiki/Vincenc_Lesn%C3%BD" title="Vincenc LesnÃ½">Vincenc LesnÃ½</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECameron2006_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron2006-172">[168]</a></sup> French Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Gide" title="AndrÃ© Gide">AndrÃ© Gide</a>, Russian poet <a href="/wiki/Anna_Akhmatova" title="Anna Akhmatova">Anna Akhmatova</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen200690_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen200690-173">[169]</a></sup> former Turkish Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BClent_Ecevit" title="BÃ¼lent Ecevit">BÃ¼lent Ecevit</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinzer2006_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinzer2006-174">[170]</a></sup> and others. In the United States, Tagore's lecturing circuits, particularly those of 1916â1917, were widely attended and wildly acclaimed. Some controversies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[e]</a></sup> involving Tagore, possibly fictive, trashed his popularity and sales in Japan and North America after the late 1920s, concluding with his "near total eclipse" outside Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen1997_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen1997-6">[6]</a></sup> Yet a latent reverence of Tagore was discovered by an astonished <a href="/wiki/Salman_Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie">Salman Rushdie</a> during a trip to Nicaragua.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995255_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995255-181">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>By way of translations, Tagore influenced Chileans <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Neruda" title="Pablo Neruda">Pablo Neruda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gabriela_Mistral" title="Gabriela Mistral">Gabriela Mistral</a>; Mexican writer <a href="/wiki/Octavio_Paz" title="Octavio Paz">Octavio Paz</a>; and Spaniards <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ortega_y_Gasset" title="JosÃ© Ortega y Gasset">JosÃ© Ortega y Gasset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zenobia_Camprub%C3%AD" title="Zenobia CamprubÃ­">Zenobia CamprubÃ­</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Juan_Ram%C3%B3n_Jim%C3%A9nez" title="Juan RamÃ³n JimÃ©nez">Juan RamÃ³n JimÃ©nez</a>. In the period 1914â1922, the JimÃ©nez-CamprubÃ­ pair produced twenty-two Spanish translations of Tagore's English corpus; they heavily revised <i>The Crescent Moon</i> and other key titles. In these years, JimÃ©nez developed "naked poetry".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995254â255_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995254â255-182">[177]</a></sup> Ortega y Gasset wrote that "Tagore's wide appeal [owes to how] he speaks of longings for perfection that we all haveÂ [...] Tagore awakens a dormant sense of childish wonder, and he saturates the air with all kinds of enchanting promises for the reader, whoÂ [...] pays little attention to the deeper import of Oriental mysticism". Tagore's works circulated in free editions around 1920âalongside those of <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">Cervantes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Tolstoy</a>.
</p><p>Tagore was deemed over-rated by some. <a href="/wiki/Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene">Graham Greene</a> doubted that "anyone but Mr. Yeats can still take his poems very seriously." Several prominent Western admirersâincluding Pound and, to a lesser extent, even Yeatsâcriticised Tagore's work. Yeats, unimpressed with his English translations, railed against that "Damn TagoreÂ [...] We got out three good books, Sturge Moore and I, and then, because he thought it more important to see and know English than to be a great poet, he brought out sentimental rubbish and wrecked his reputation. Tagore does not know English, no Indian knows English."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen1997_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen1997-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya2001_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya2001-183">[178]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Radice" title="William Radice">William Radice</a>, who "English[ed]" his poems, asked: "What is their place in world literature?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200426_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200426-184">[179]</a></sup> He saw him as "kind of counter-cultur[al]", bearing "a new kind of classicism" that would heal the "collapsed romantic confusion and chaos of the 20th [c]entury."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya2001_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya2001-183">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200426â31_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200426â31-185">[180]</a></sup> The translated Tagore was "almost nonsensical",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200418â19_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreRadice200418â19-186">[181]</a></sup> and subpar English offerings reduced his trans-national appeal:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Anyone who knows Tagore's poems in their original Bengali cannot feel satisfied with any of the translations (made with or without Yeats's help). Even the translations of his prose works suffer, to some extent, from distortion. E.M. Forster noted [of] <i>The Home and the World</i> [that] '[t]he theme is so beautiful,' but the charms have 'vanished in translation,' or perhaps 'in an experiment that has not quite come off.'</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a>, "Tagore and His India".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen1997_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen1997-6">[6]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums">Museums</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interior_room,_Jorasanko_Mansion,_Kolkata,_India.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Interior_room%2C_Jorasanko_Mansion%2C_Kolkata%2C_India.jpg/170px-Interior_room%2C_Jorasanko_Mansion%2C_Kolkata%2C_India.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Interior_room%2C_Jorasanko_Mansion%2C_Kolkata%2C_India.jpg/255px-Interior_room%2C_Jorasanko_Mansion%2C_Kolkata%2C_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Interior_room%2C_Jorasanko_Mansion%2C_Kolkata%2C_India.jpg/340px-Interior_room%2C_Jorasanko_Mansion%2C_Kolkata%2C_India.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interior_room,_Jorasanko_Mansion,_Kolkata,_India.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jorasanko_Thakur_Bari" title="Jorasanko Thakur Bari">Jorasanko Thakur Bari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a>; the room in which Tagore died in 1941.</div></div></div>

<p>There are eight Tagore museums, three in India and five in Bangladesh:
</p>
<ul><li>Rabindra Bharati Museum, at <a href="/wiki/Jorasanko_Thakur_Bari" title="Jorasanko Thakur Bari">Jorasanko Thakur Bari</a>, Kolkata, India</li>
<li>Tagore Memorial Museum, at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shilaidaha_Kuthibadi" title="Shilaidaha Kuthibadi">Shilaidaha Kuthibadi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shilaidaha" title="Shilaidaha">Shilaidaha</a>, Bangladesh</li>
<li>Rabindra Memorial Museum at <a href="/wiki/Shahzadpur_Kachharibari" title="Shahzadpur Kachharibari">Shahzadpur Kachharibari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shahjadpur_Upazila" title="Shahjadpur Upazila">Shahzadpur</a>, Bangladesh</li>
<li>Rabindra Bhavan Museum, in <a href="/wiki/Santiniketan" title="Santiniketan">Santiniketan</a>, India</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rabindra_Museum" title="Rabindra Museum">Rabindra Museum</a>, in Mungpoo, near Kalimpong, India</li>
<li>Patisar Rabindra Kacharibari, Patisar, <a href="/wiki/Atrai_Upazila" title="Atrai Upazila">Atrai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naogaon_District" title="Naogaon District">Naogaon</a>, Bangladesh</li>
<li>Pithavoge Rabindra Memorial Complex, Pithavoge, <a href="/wiki/Rupsa_Upazila" title="Rupsa Upazila">Rupsha</a>, Khulna, Bangladesh</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rabindra_Complex" title="Rabindra Complex">Rabindra Complex</a>, Dakkhindihi village, <a href="/wiki/Phultala_Upazila" title="Phultala Upazila">Phultala Upazila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khulna" title="Khulna">Khulna</a>, Bangladesh</li></ul>

title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rabindra_Complex" title="Rabindra Complex">Rabindra Complex</a>, Dakkhindihi, Phultala, Khulna, Bangladesh</div></div></div>
<p>Jorasanko Thakur Bari (<a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>: <i>House of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thakur_(Indian_title)" title="Thakur (Indian title)">Thakurs</a></i>; anglicised to <i>Tagore</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Jorasanko" title="Jorasanko">Jorasanko</a>, north of Kolkata, is the ancestral home of the Tagore family. It is currently located on the <a href="/wiki/Rabindra_Bharati_University" title="Rabindra Bharati University">Rabindra Bharati University</a> campus at 6/4 Dwarakanath Tagore Lane<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[182]</a></sup> Jorasanko, Kolkata 700007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[183]</a></sup> It is the house in which Tagore was born, and also the place where he spent most of his childhood and where he died on 7 August 1941.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rabindra_Complex" title="Rabindra Complex">Rabindra Complex</a> is located in Dakkhindihi village, near <a href="/wiki/Phultala_Upazila" title="Phultala Upazila">Phultala Upazila</a>, 19 kilometres (12Â mi) from <a href="/wiki/Khulna" title="Khulna">Khulna</a> city, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>. It was the residence of Tagore's father-in-law, Beni Madhab Roy Chowdhury. The Tagore family had close connection with Dakkhindihi village. The maternal ancestral home of the great poet was also situated at Dakkhindihi village; the poet's mother Sarada Sundari Devi and his paternal aunt by marriage Tripura Sundari Devi were born in this village. Young Tagore used to visit Dakkhindihi village with his mother to visit his maternal uncles in her mother's ancestral home; Tagore visited this place several times in his life. It has been declared as a protected archaeological site by the Department of Archaeology of Bangladesh and converted into a museum. In 1995, the local administration took charge of the house and on 14 November of that year, the Rabindra Complex project was decided. The Department of Archeology carried out the renovation work to make the house a museum titled âRabindra Complexâ in the 2011â12 fiscal year. The two-storey museum building has four rooms on the first floor and two rooms on the ground floor at present. The building has eight windows on the ground floor and 21 windows on the first floor. The height of the roof from the floor on the ground floor is 13 feet. There are seven doors, six windows and wall almirahs on the first floor. Over 500 books were kept in the library and all the rooms have been decorated with rare pictures of Rabindranath. Over 10,000 visitors come here every year to see the museum from different parts of the country and also from abroad, said Saifur Rahman, assistant director of the Department of Archeology in Khulna. A bust of Rabindranath Tagore is also there. Every year on 25â27 Baishakh (after the Bengali New Year Celebration), cultural programs are held here that last for three days.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:20%; ; font-size: 85%;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<div class="poem">
<p>Who are you, reader, reading my poems a hundred years hence?<br/>
I cannot send you one single flower from this wealth of the spring, one single streak of gold from yonder clouds.<br/>
Open your doors and look abroad.<br/>
From your blossoming garden gather fragrant memories of the vanished flowers of an hundred years before.<br/>
In the joy of your heart may you feel the living joy that sang one spring morning, sending its glad voice across an hundred years.
</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">â <i>The Gardener</i>, 1915.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagoreRay2007104_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagoreRay2007104-189">[184]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_works">List of works</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_by_Rabindranath_Tagore" title="List of works by Rabindranath Tagore">List of works by Rabindranath Tagore</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore_filmography" title="Rabindranath Tagore filmography">Rabindranath Tagore filmography</a></div>
<p>The SNLTR hosts the 1415 BE edition of Tagore's complete Bengali works. Tagore Web also hosts an edition of Tagore's works, including annotated songs. Translations are found at Project Gutenberg and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Author:Rabindranath_Tagore" title="s:Author:Rabindranath Tagore">Wikisource</a>. More sources are <a href="#External_links">below</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations_of_novels_and_short_stories_in_cinema">Adaptations of novels and short stories in cinema</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Adaptations_of_works_of_Rabindranath_Tagore_in_film_and_television" title="Adaptations of works of Rabindranath Tagore in film and television">Adaptations of works of Rabindranath Tagore in film and television</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bengali">Bengali</span></h3>
<ul><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natir_Puja_(film)" title="Natir Puja (film)">Natir Puja</a></i> â 1932 â The only film directed by Rabindranath Tagore</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Gora_(film)" title="Gora (film)">Gora</a></i> â 1938 <a href="/wiki/Gora_(novel)" title="Gora (novel)">Gora (novel)</a> â <a href="/wiki/Naresh_Mitra" title="Naresh Mitra">Naresh Mitra</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Noukadubi_(1947_film)" title="Noukadubi (1947 film)">Noukadubi</a></i> â <a href="/wiki/Nitin_Bose" title="Nitin Bose">Nitin Bose</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bou_Thakuranir_Haat" title="Bou Thakuranir Haat">Bou Thakuranir Haat</a></i> â 1953 (<i>Bou Thakuranir Haat</i>) â <a href="/wiki/Naresh_Mitra" title="Naresh Mitra">Naresh Mitra</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kabuliwala_(1957_film)" title="Kabuliwala (1957 film)">Kabuliwala</a></i> â 1957 (Kabuliwala) â <a href="/wiki/Tapan_Sinha" title="Tapan Sinha">Tapan Sinha</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kshudhita_Pashan" title="Kshudhita Pashan">Kshudhita Pashan</a></i> â 1960 (Kshudhita Pashan) â Tapan Sinha</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Teen_Kanya" title="Teen Kanya">Teen Kanya</a></i> â 1961 (Teen Kanya) â <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore_(film)" title="Rabindranath Tagore (film)">Rabindranath Tagore</a></i> is a 1961 Indian documentary film written and directed by <a href="/wiki/Satyajit_Ray" title="Satyajit Ray">Satyajit Ray</a>, released during the birth centenary of Tagore. It was produced by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">Government of India</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Films_Division" title="Films Division">Films Division</a>.</li>
<li>Serbian composer <a href="/wiki/Darinka_Simic-Mitrovic" title="Darinka Simic-Mitrovic">Darinka Simic-Mitrovic</a> used Tagoreâs text for her song cycle <i>Gradinar</i> in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[186]</a></sup></li>
<li>In Sukanta Roy's <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_West_Bengal" title="Cinema of West Bengal">Bengali</a> film <i>Chhelebela</i> (2002) <a href="/wiki/Jisshu_Sengupta" title="Jisshu Sengupta">Jisshu Sengupta</a> portrayed Tagore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[187]</a></sup></li>
<li>In Bandana Mukhopadhyay's Bengali film <i>Chirosakha He</i> (2007) Sayandip Bhattacharya played Tagore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[188]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Rituparno_Ghosh" title="Rituparno Ghosh">Rituparno Ghosh</a>'s Bengali documentary film <i>Jeevan Smriti</i> (2011) <a href="/wiki/Samadarshi_Dutta" title="Samadarshi Dutta">Samadarshi Dutta</a> played Tagore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Suman_Ghosh_(director)" title="Suman Ghosh (director)">Suman Ghosh</a>'s Bengali film <i><a href="/wiki/Kadambari_(2015_film)" title="Kadambari (2015 film)">Kadambari</a></i> (2015) <a href="/wiki/Parambrata_Chatterjee" title="Parambrata Chatterjee">Parambrata Chatterjee</a> portrayed Tagore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[190]</a></sup></li></ul>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Gurudev</i> translates as "divine mentor", <i>Bishokobi</i> translates as "poet of the world" and <i>Kobiguru</i> translates as "great poet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESil2005_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESil2005-8">[8]</a></sup>Â </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tagore was born at No. 6 Dwarkanath Tagore Lane, Jorasanko â the address of the main mansion (the <i>Jorasanko Thakurbari</i>) inhabited by the Jorasanko branch of the Tagore clan, which had earlier suffered an acrimonious split. Jorasanko was located in the Bengali section of Calcutta, near Chitpur Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199534_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199534-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199537_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson199537-23">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dwarkanath_Tagore" title="Dwarkanath Tagore">Dwarkanath Tagore</a> was his paternal grandfather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_News_Today2011_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_News_Today2011-24">[23]</a></sup> Debendranath had formulated the <a href="/wiki/Brahmo" title="Brahmo">Brahmoist</a> philosophies espoused by his friend <a href="/wiki/Ram_Mohan_Roy" title="Ram Mohan Roy">Ram Mohan Roy</a>, and became focal in Brahmo society after Roy's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoy197728â30_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoy197728â30-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETagore1997b8â9_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETagore1997b8â9-26">[25]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On the "idea of the humanity of our God, or the divinity of Man the Eternal".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-158">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Etymology of "Visva-Bharati": from the Sanskrit for "world" or "universe" and the name of a Rigvedic goddess ("Bharati") associated with <a href="/wiki/Saraswati" title="Saraswati">Saraswati</a>, the Hindu patron of learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995220_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995220-157">[154]</a></sup> "Visva-Bharati" also translates as "India in the World".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-180"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-180">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tagore was no stranger to controversy: his dealings with Indian nationalists <a href="/wiki/Subhas_Chandra_Bose" title="Subhas Chandra Bose">Subhas Chandra Bose</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESen1997_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESen1997-6">[6]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rash_Behari_Bose" title="Rash Behari Bose">Rash Behari Bose</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995214_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995214-175">[171]</a></sup> his yen for Soviet Communism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995297_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995297-176">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995214â215_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995214â215-177">[173]</a></sup> and papers confiscated from Indian nationalists in New York allegedly implicating Tagore in a plot to overthrow the Raj via German funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995212_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995212-178">[174]</a></sup> These destroyed Tagore's imageâand book salesâin the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995214_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995214-175">[171]</a></sup> His relations with and ambivalent opinion of Mussolini revolted many;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKundu2009_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKundu2009-93">[91]</a></sup> close friend <a href="/wiki/Romain_Rolland" title="Romain Rolland">Romain Rolland</a> despaired that "[h]e is abdicating his role as moral guide of the independent spirits of Europe and India".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995273_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuttaRobinson1995273-179">[175]</a></sup></span>
</li>
